<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Hurricane" redirects here. For the Filipino band, see <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Depression_(band)" title="Tropical Depression (band)">Tropical Depression (band)</a>.</div>

.navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output <p>A <b>tropical cyclone</b> is a rapidly rotating <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storm system</a> characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure</a> center, a closed low-level <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">strong winds</a>, and a spiral arrangement of <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> that produce heavy rain and <a href="/wiki/Squall" title="Squall">squalls</a>. Depending on its location and strength, a tropical cyclone is referred to by <a href="#Nomenclature_and_intensity_classifications">different names</a>, including <b>hurricane</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ʌr/: 'urr' in 'hurry'">ʌr</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>), <b>typhoon</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>), <b>tropical storm</b>, <b>cyclonic storm</b>, <b>tropical depression</b>, or simply <b>cyclone</b>:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane" title="Atlantic hurricane">hurricane</a> is a strong tropical cyclone that occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> or northeastern <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhoon</a> occurs in the northwestern Pacific Ocean. In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, south Pacific, or (rarely) South Atlantic, comparable storms are referred to simply as "tropical cyclones", and such storms in the Indian Ocean can also be called "severe cyclonic storms".
</p><p>"Tropical" refers to the geographical origin of these systems, which form almost exclusively over <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> seas. "Cyclone" refers to their winds moving in a circle, whirling round their central clear <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a>, with their surface winds blowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clockwise_and_counterclockwise" title="Clockwise and counterclockwise">counterclockwise</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clockwise" title="Clockwise">clockwise</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. The opposite direction of circulation is due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>. Tropical cyclones <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis" title="Tropical cyclogenesis">typically form</a> over large bodies of relatively warm water. They derive their energy through the evaporation of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> surface, which ultimately <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condenses</a> into <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> and rain when moist air rises and cools to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturated_fluid" title="Saturated fluid">saturation</a>. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_source" title="Energy source">energy source</a> differs from that of <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">mid-latitude cyclonic storms</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Nor%27easter" title="Nor'easter">nor'easters</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_windstorm" title="European windstorm">European windstorms</a>, which are powered primarily by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baroclinic_instability" title="Baroclinic instability">horizontal temperature contrasts</a>. Tropical cyclones are typically between 100 and 2,000 km (62 and 1,243 mi) in diameter. Every year tropical cyclones impact various regions of the globe including the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="Gulf Coast of the United States">Gulf Coast of North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.
</p><p>The strong rotating winds of a tropical cyclone are a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a> imparted by the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">Earth's rotation</a> as air flows inwards toward the axis of rotation. As a result, they rarely form within 5° of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Tropical cyclones are very rare in the South Atlantic (although <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone" title="South Atlantic tropical cyclone">occasional examples do occur</a>) due to consistently strong <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> and a weak <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>. Conversely, the <a href="/wiki/African_easterly_jet" title="African easterly jet">African easterly jet</a> and areas of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability" title="Atmospheric instability">atmospheric instability</a> give rise to cyclones in the Atlantic Ocean and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, while cyclones near Australia owe their genesis to <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_of_South_Asia" title="Monsoon of South Asia">the Asian monsoon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Pacific_Warm_Pool" title="Western Pacific Warm Pool">Western Pacific Warm Pool</a>.
</p><p>The primary energy source for these storms is warm ocean waters. These storms are therefore typically strongest when over or near water, and they weaken quite rapidly over land. This causes coastal regions to be particularly vulnerable to tropical cyclones, compared to inland regions. Coastal damage may be caused by strong winds and rain, high waves (due to winds), <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surges</a> (due to wind and severe pressure changes), and <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">the potential</a> of spawning <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>. Tropical cyclones draw in air from a large area and concentrate the water content of that air (from atmospheric moisture and moisture evaporated from water) into <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> over a much smaller area. This replenishing of moisture-bearing air after rain may cause multi-hour or multi-day extremely heavy rain up to 40 km (25 mi) from the coastline, far beyond the amount of water that the local atmosphere holds at any one time. This in turn can lead to river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding">flooding</a>, overland flooding, and a general overwhelming of local water control structures across a large area. Although their effects on human populations can be devastating, tropical cyclones may play a role in relieving <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> conditions, though this claim is disputed. They also carry heat and energy away from the tropics and transport it towards temperate latitudes, which plays an important role in regulating global climate.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<p>A tropical cyclone is the generic term for a warm-cored, non-frontal <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology" title="Synoptic scale meteorology">synoptic-scale</a> <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure system</a> over <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical</a> waters around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gloss_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gloss-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-What-2">[2]</a></sup> The systems generally have a well-defined center which is surrounded by deep <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">atmospheric convection</a> and a closed wind circulation at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gloss_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gloss-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, tropical cyclones have occurred around the world for thousands of years, with one of the earliest tropical cyclones on record estimated to have occurred in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> in around 4000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6000_WA_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-6000_WA-3">[3]</a></sup> However, before satellite imagery became available during the 20th century, many of these systems went undetected unless it impacted land or a ship encountered it by chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>These days, on average around 80 to 90 named tropical cyclones form each year around the world, over half of which develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane-force_winds" title="Hurricane-force winds">hurricane-force winds</a> of 65 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (120 km/h; 75 mph) or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Around the world, a tropical cyclone is generally deemed to have formed once mean surface winds in excess of 35 kn (65 km/h; 40 mph) are observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> It is assumed at this stage that a tropical cyclone has become self-sustaining and can continue to intensify without any help from its environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study review article published in 2021 in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Geoscience" title="Nature Geoscience">Nature Geoscience</a></i> concluded that the geographic range of tropical cyclones will probably expand poleward in response to climate warming of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadley_circulation" title="Hadley circulation">Hadley circulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity">Intensity</span></h2>
<p>Tropical cyclone intensity is based on wind speeds and pressure; relationships between winds and pressure are often utilized in determining the intensity of a storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Tropical cyclone scales such as the Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Wind Scale and Australia's scale (Bureau of Meteorology) only use wind speed for determining the category of a storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The most intense storm on record is <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Tip" title="Typhoon Tip">Typhoon Tip</a> in the northwestern Pacific Ocean in 1979, which reached a minimum pressure of 870 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a> (26 <a href="/wiki/Inch_of_mercury" title="Inch of mercury">inHg</a>) and maximum sustained wind speeds of 165 kn (85 m/s; 306 km/h; 190 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jtwc_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-jtwc-9">[9]</a></sup> The highest maximum sustained wind speed ever recorded was 185 kn (95 m/s; 343 km/h; 213 mph) in <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Patricia" title="Hurricane Patricia">Hurricane Patricia</a> in 2015—the most intense cyclone ever recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_that_influence_intensity">Factors that influence intensity</span></h3>
<p>Warm <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperatures</a> are required in order for tropical cyclones to form and strengthen. The commonly-accepted minimum temperature range for this to occur is 26–27 °C (79–81 °F), however, multiple studies have proposed a lower minimum of 25.5 °C (77.9 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Higher sea surface temperatures result in faster intensification rates and sometimes even <a href="/wiki/Rapid_intensification" title="Rapid intensification">rapid intensification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> High <a href="/wiki/Ocean_heat_content" title="Ocean heat content">ocean heat content</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Cyclone_Heat_Potential" title="Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential">Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential</a>, allows storms to achieve a higher intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_Intensity_Forecasting_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_Intensity_Forecasting-14">[14]</a></sup> Most tropical cyclones that experience rapid intensification are traversing regions of high ocean heat content rather than lower values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OHC_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-OHC-15">[15]</a></sup> High ocean heat content values can help to offset the oceanic cooling caused by the passage of a tropical cyclone, limiting the effect this cooling has on the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Faster-moving systems are able to intensify to higher intensities with lower ocean heat content values. Slower-moving systems require higher values of ocean heat content to achieve the same intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OHC_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-OHC-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The passage of a tropical cyclone over the ocean causes the upper layers of the ocean to cool substantially, a process known as <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> which can negatively influence subsequent cyclone development. This cooling is primarily caused by wind-driven mixing of cold water from deeper in the ocean with the warm surface waters. This effect results in a negative feedback process that can inhibit further development or lead to weakening. Additional cooling may come in the form of cold water from falling raindrops (this is because the atmosphere is cooler at higher altitudes). Cloud cover may also play a role in cooling the ocean, by shielding the ocean surface from direct sunlight before and slightly after the storm passage. All these effects can combine to produce a dramatic drop in sea surface temperature over a large area in just a few days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Cooling_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Cooling-18">[18]</a></sup> Conversely, the mixing of the sea can result in heat being inserted in deeper waters, with <a href="/wiki/Cyclonic_Ni%C3%B1o" title="Cyclonic Niño">potential effects</a> on global <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wind_shear#Vertical_component" title="Wind shear">Vertical wind shear</a> negatively impacts tropical cyclone intensification by displacing moisture and heat from a system's center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Low levels of vertical wind shear are most optimal for strengthening, while stronger wind shear induces weakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Dry air entraining into a tropical cyclone's core has a negative effect on its development and intensity by diminishing atmospheric convection and introducing asymmetries in the storm's structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Symmetric, strong <a href="/wiki/Outflow_(meteorology)" title="Outflow (meteorology)">outflow</a> leads to a faster rate of intensification than observed in other systems by mitigating local wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Weakening outflow is associated with the weakening of <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">rainbands</a> within a tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of tropical cyclones plays a role in how quickly they intensify. Smaller tropical cyclones are more prone to rapid intensification than larger ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fujiwhara_effect" title="Fujiwhara effect">Fujiwhara effect</a>, which involves interaction between two tropical cyclones, can weaken and ultimately result in the dissipation of the weaker of two tropical cyclones by reducing the organization of the system's convection and imparting horizontal wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones typically weaken while situated over a landmass because conditions are often unfavorable as a result of the lack of oceanic forcing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brown_ocean_effect" title="Brown ocean effect">Brown ocean effect</a> can allow a tropical cyclone to maintain or increase its intensity following <a href="/wiki/Landfall" title="Landfall">landfall</a>, in cases where there has been copious rainfall, through the release of latent heat from the saturated soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">Orographic lift</a> can cause an significant increase in the intensity of the convection of a tropical cyclone when its eye moves over a mountain, breaking the capped boundary layer that had been restraining it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Jet streams can both enhance and inhibit tropical cyclone intensity by influencing the storm's outflow as well as vertical wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis" title="Tropical cyclogenesis">Tropical cyclogenesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane-en.svg"><img alt="A schematic diagram of a tropical cyclone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Hurricane-en.svg/290px-Hurricane-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Hurricane-en.svg/435px-Hurricane-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Hurricane-en.svg/580px-Hurricane-en.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of a tropical cyclone in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tropical cyclones tend to develop during the summer, but have been noted in nearly every month in most <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins" title="Tropical cyclone basins">tropical cyclone basins</a>. Tropical cyclones on either side of the Equator generally have their origins in the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>, where winds blow from either the northeast or southeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Form-37">[37]</a></sup> Within this broad area of low-pressure, air is heated over the warm tropical ocean and rises in discrete parcels, which causes thundery showers to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Form-37">[37]</a></sup> These showers dissipate quite quickly; however, they can group together into large clusters of thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Form-37">[37]</a></sup> This creates a flow of warm, moist, rapidly rising air, which starts to <a href="/wiki/Cyclonic_rotation" title="Cyclonic rotation">rotate cyclonically</a> as it interacts with the rotation of the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-Form-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several factors are required for these thunderstorms to develop further, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperatures" title="Sea surface temperatures">sea surface temperatures</a> of around 27 °C (81 °F) and low vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> surrounding the system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-Form-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A15_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-A15-38">[38]</a></sup> atmospheric instability, high <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> in the lower to middle levels of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, enough <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a> to develop a <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure center</a>, a pre-existing low-level focus or disturbance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A15_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-A15-38">[38]</a></sup>
There is a limit on tropical cyclone intensity which is strongly related to the water temperatures along its path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSMAS_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-RSMAS-39">[39]</a></sup> and upper-level divergence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
An average of 86 tropical cyclones of tropical storm intensity form annually worldwide. Of those, 47 reach strength higher than 119 km/h (74 mph), and 20 become intense tropical cyclones (at least Category 3 intensity on the <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">Saffir–Simpson scale</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">Climate</a> cycles such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ENSO" title="ENSO">ENSO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Madden%E2%80%93Julian_oscillation" title="Madden–Julian oscillation">Madden–Julian oscillation</a> modulate the timing and frequency of tropical cyclone development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_climt_var_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_climt_var-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rossby_wave" title="Rossby wave">Rossby waves</a> can aid in the formation of a new tropical cyclone by disseminating the energy of an existing, mature storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_wave" title="Kelvin wave">Kelvin waves</a> can contribute to tropical cyclone formation by regulating the development of the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Cyclone formation is usually reduced 3 days prior to the wave's crest and increased during the 3 days after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rapid_intensification">Rapid intensification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rapid_intensification" title="Rapid intensification">Rapid intensification</a></div>
<p>On occasion, tropical cyclones may undergo a process known as rapid intensification, a period in which the maximum sustained winds of a tropical cyclone increase by 30 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (56 km/h; 35 mph) or more within 24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_Glossary_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS_Glossary-50">[50]</a></sup> Similarly, rapid deepening in tropical cyclones is defined as a minimum sea surface pressure decrease of 1.75 hPa (0.052 inHg) per hour or 42 hPa (1.2 inHg) within a 24-hour period; explosive deepening occurs when the surface pressure decreases by 2.5 hPa (0.074 inHg) per hour for at least 12 hours or 5 hPa (0.15 inHg) per hour for at least 6 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> For rapid intensification to occur, several conditions must be in place. Water temperatures must be extremely high (near or above 30 °C (86 °F)), and water of this temperature must be sufficiently deep such that waves do not upwell cooler waters to the surface. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Cyclone_Heat_Potential" title="Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential">Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential</a> is one of such non-conventional subsurface <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanographic</a> parameters influencing the <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclone</a> intensity. <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">Wind shear</a> must be low; when wind shear is high, the <a href="/wiki/Convection#Atmospheric_convection" title="Convection">convection</a> and circulation in the cyclone will be disrupted. Usually, an <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anticyclone</a> in the upper layers of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> above the storm must be present as well—for extremely low surface pressures to develop, air must be rising very rapidly in the eyewall of the storm, and an upper-level anticyclone helps channel this air away from the cyclone efficiently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energetics_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Energetics-52">[52]</a></sup> However, some cyclones such as <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Epsilon_(2020)" title="Hurricane Epsilon (2020)">Hurricane Epsilon</a> have rapidly intensified despite relatively unfavorable conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissipation">Dissipation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png"><img alt="Satellite image of a cyclone where the thickest clouds are displaced from the central vortex" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3500" data-file-width="3500" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png/220px-Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png/330px-Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png/440px-Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paulette_2020-09-10_1200Z.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane_Paulette_(2020)" title="Hurricane Paulette (2020)">Hurricane Paulette</a>, in <a href="/wiki/2020_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2020 Atlantic hurricane season">2020</a>, is an example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windshear" title="Windshear">sheared</a> tropical cyclone, with deep <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convection</a> slightly removed from the center of the system.</div></div></div>
<p>There are a number of ways a tropical cyclone can weaken, dissipate, or lose its tropical characteristics. These include making landfall, moving over cooler water, encountering dry air, or interacting with other weather systems; however, once a system has dissipated or lost its tropical characteristics, its remnants could regenerate a tropical cyclone if environmental conditions become favorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HurricaneGraveyard_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-HurricaneGraveyard-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler_and_Kilonsky_1977_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler_and_Kilonsky_1977-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>A tropical cyclone can dissipate when it moves over waters significantly cooler than 26.5 °C (79.7 °F). This will deprive the storm of such tropical characteristics as a warm core with thunderstorms near the center, so that it becomes a remnant <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure area</a>. Remnant systems may persist for several days before losing their identity. This dissipation mechanism is most common in the eastern North Pacific. Weakening or dissipation can also occur if a storm experiences vertical wind shear which causes the convection and heat engine to move away from the center; this normally ceases the development of a tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAM_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-EAM-57">[57]</a></sup> In addition, its interaction with the main belt of the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a>, by means of merging with a nearby frontal zone, can cause tropical cyclones to evolve into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclones" title="Extratropical cyclones">extratropical cyclones</a>. This transition can take 1–3 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JWTC_intensity_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-JWTC_intensity-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Should a tropical cyclone make landfall or pass over an island, its circulation could start to break down, especially if it encounters mountainous terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeCycle_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-LifeCycle-59">[59]</a></sup> When a system makes landfall on a large landmass, it is cut off from its supply of warm moist maritime air and starts to draw in dry continental air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeCycle_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-LifeCycle-59">[59]</a></sup> This, combined with the increased friction over land areas, leads to the weakening and dissipation of the tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeCycle_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-LifeCycle-59">[59]</a></sup> Over a mountainous terrain, a system can quickly weaken; however, over flat areas, it may endure for two to three days before circulation breaks down and dissipates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeCycle_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-LifeCycle-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the years, there have been a number of techniques considered to try to artificially modify tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stop_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stop-60">[60]</a></sup> These techniques have included using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, cooling the ocean with icebergs, blowing the storm away from land with giant fans, and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">seeding</a> selected storms with dry ice or <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stop_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stop-60">[60]</a></sup> These techniques, however, fail to appreciate the duration, intensity, power or size of tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stop_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stop-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_for_assessing_intensity">Methods for assessing intensity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For broader coverage of this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Dvorak_technique" title="Dvorak technique">Dvorak technique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scatterometer" title="Scatterometer">Scatterometer</a>.</div>
<p>A variety of methods or techniques, including surface, satellite, and aerial, are utilized to assess the intensity of a tropical cyclone. Reconnaissance aircraft fly around and through tropical cyclones, outfitted with specialized instruments, to collect information that can be used to ascertain the winds and pressure of a system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones possess winds of different speeds at different heights. Winds recorded at flight level can be converted to find the wind speeds at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Surface observations, such as ship reports, land stations, <a href="/wiki/Mesonet" title="Mesonet">mesonets</a>, coastal stations, and buoys, can provide information on a tropical cyclone's intensity or the direction it is traveling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Wind-pressure relationships (WPRs) are used as a way to determine the pressure of a storm based on its wind speed. Several different methods and equations have been proposed to calculate WPRs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPRs_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPRs-63">[63]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones agencies each use their own, fixed WPR, which can result in inaccuracies between agencies that are issuing estimates on the same system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPRs_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPRs-63">[63]</a></sup> The ASCAT is a <a href="/wiki/Scatterometer" title="Scatterometer">scatterometer</a> used by the <a href="/wiki/MetOp" title="MetOp">MetOp</a> satellites to map the wind field vectors of tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> The SMAP uses an L-band <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" title="Radiometer">radiometer</a> channel to determine the wind speeds of tropical cyclones at the ocean surface, and has been shown to be reliable at higher intensities and under heavy rainfall conditions, unlike scatterometer-based and other radiometer-based instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dvorak_technique" title="Dvorak technique">Dvorak technique</a> plays a large role in both the classification of a tropical cyclone and the determination of its intensity. Used in warning centers, the method was developed by <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Dvorak" title="Vernon Dvorak">Vernon Dvorak</a> in the 1970s, and uses both visible and infrared satellite imagery in the assessment of tropical cyclone intensity. The Dvorak technique utilizes a scale of "T-numbers", scaling in increments of ½ from T1.0 to T8.0. Each T-number has an intensity assigned to it, with larger T-numbers indicating a stronger system. Tropical cyclones are assessed by forecasters according to an array of patterns, including <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">curved banding features</a>, shear, central dense overcast, and eye, in order to determine the T-number and thus assess the intensity of the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_Institute_for_Meteorological_Satellite_Studies" title="Cooperative Institute for Meteorological Satellite Studies">Cooperative Institute for Meteorological Satellite Studies</a> works to develop and improve automated satellite methods, such as the Advanced Dvorak Technique (ADT) and SATCON. The ADT, used by a large number of forecasting centers, utilizes infrared geostationary satellite imagery and an algorithm based upon the Dvorak technique to assess the intensity of tropical cyclones. The ADT has a number of differences from the conventional Dvorak technique, including changes to intensity constraint rules and the usage of microwave imagery to base a system's intensity upon its internal structure, which prevents the intensity from leveling off before an eye emerges in infrared imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The SATCON weights estimates from various satellite-based systems and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_microwave_sounding_unit" title="Advanced microwave sounding unit">microwave sounders</a>, accounting for the strengths and flaws in each individual estimate, to produce a consensus estimate of a tropical cyclone's intensity which can be more reliable than the Dvorak technique at times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity_metrics">Intensity metrics</span></h3>
<p>Multiple intensity metrics are utilized, including <a href="/wiki/Accumulated_cyclone_energy" title="Accumulated cyclone energy">accumulated cyclone energy</a> (ACE), the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Surge_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hurricane Surge Index (page does not exist)">Hurricane Surge Index</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Severity_Index" title="Hurricane Severity Index">Hurricane Severity Index</a>, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Power_Dissipation_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Power Dissipation Index (page does not exist)">Power Dissipation Index</a> (PDI), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Integrated_kinetic_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Integrated kinetic energy (page does not exist)">integrated kinetic energy</a> (IKE). ACE is a metric of the total energy a system has exerted over its lifespan. ACE is calculated by summing the squares of a cyclone's sustained wind speed, every six hours as long as the system is at or above tropical storm intensity and either tropical or subtropical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The calculation of the PDI is similar in nature to ACE, with the major difference being that wind speeds are cubed rather than squared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The Hurricane Surge Index is a metric of the potential damage a storm may inflict via storm surge. It is calculated by squaring the dividend of the storm's wind speed and a climatological value (33 metres per second (74 mph)), and then multiplying that quantity by the dividend of the radius of hurricane-force winds and its climatological value (96.6 kilometres (60.0 mi)). This can be represented in equation form as:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {v}{33m/s}}\right)^{2}\times \left({\frac {r}{96.6km}}\right)\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where v is the storm's wind speed and r is the radius of hurricane-force winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The Hurricane Severity Index is a scale that can assign up to 50 points to a system; up to 25 points come from intensity, while the other 25 come from the size of the storm's wind field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IKE_+_HSI_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-IKE_+_HSI-72">[72]</a></sup> The IKE model measures the destructive capability of a tropical cyclone via winds, waves, and surge. It is calculated as:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \int _{Vol}{\frac {1}{2}}pu^{2}d_{v}\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \int _{Vol}{\frac {1}{2}}pu^{2}d_{v}\,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edbf3f4364293fbc62e7fae3b2e8f429ccb3b0ac" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:12.641ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where p is the density of air, u is a sustained surface wind speed value, and d<sub>v</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Volume_element" title="Volume element">volume element</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IKE_+_HSI_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-IKE_+_HSI-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_naming">Classification and naming</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity_classifications">Intensity classifications</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_scales" title="Tropical cyclone scales">Tropical cyclone scales</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maria,_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg"><img alt="Satellite image of three simultaneous tropical cyclones" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9000" data-file-width="7200" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg/210px-Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg/315px-Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg/420px-Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maria,_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three tropical cyclones of the <a href="/wiki/2006_Pacific_typhoon_season" title="2006 Pacific typhoon season">2006 Pacific typhoon season</a> at different stages of development. <a href="/wiki/2006_Pacific_typhoon_season#Severe_Tropical_Storm_Bopha_(Inday)" title="2006 Pacific typhoon season">The weakest</a> (left) demonstrates only the most basic circular shape. <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Maria_(2006)" title="Typhoon Maria (2006)">A stronger storm</a> (top right) demonstrates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainbands" title="Rainbands">spiral banding</a> and increased centralization, while the <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Saomai" title="Typhoon Saomai">strongest</a> (lower right) has developed an <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Around the world, tropical cyclones are classified in different ways, based on the location (<a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins" title="Tropical cyclone basins">tropical cyclone basins</a>), the structure of the system and its intensity. For example, within the Northern Atlantic and Eastern Pacific basins, a tropical cyclone with wind speeds of over 65 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (120 km/h; 75 mph) is called a <b>hurricane</b>, while it is called a <b>typhoon</b> or a severe cyclonic storm within the Western Pacific or North Indian Oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATL/EPAC_TCOP_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATL/EPAC_TCOP-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPAC_TCOP_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIO_TCOP_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-76">[76]</a></sup> Within the Southern Hemisphere, it is either called a hurricane, tropical cyclone or a severe tropical cyclone, depending on if it is located within the South Atlantic, South-West Indian Ocean, Australian region or the South Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWIO_TCOP_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming" title="Tropical cyclone naming">Tropical cyclone naming</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_tropical_cyclone_naming" title="History of tropical cyclone naming">History of tropical cyclone naming</a></div>
<p>The practice of using names to identify tropical cyclones goes back many years, with systems named after places or things they hit before the formal start of naming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahina_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-80">[80]</a></sup> The system currently used provides positive identification of severe weather systems in a brief form, that is readily understood and recognized by the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahina_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-80">[80]</a></sup> The credit for the first usage of personal names for weather systems is generally given to the <a href="/wiki/Queensland_Government" title="Queensland Government">Queensland Government</a> Meteorologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clement_Wragge" title="Clement Wragge">Clement Wragge</a> who named systems between 1887 and 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahina_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-80">[80]</a></sup> This system of naming weather systems subsequently fell into disuse for several years after Wragge retired, until it was revived in the latter part of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> for the Western Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahina_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-80">[80]</a></sup> Formal naming schemes have subsequently been introduced for the North and South Atlantic, Eastern, Central, Western and Southern Pacific basins as well as the Australian region and Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahina_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>At present, tropical cyclones are officially named by one of twelve <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming" title="Tropical cyclone naming">meteorological services</a> and retain their names throughout their lifetimes to provide ease of communication between forecasters and the general public regarding forecasts, watches, and warnings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup> Since the systems can last a week or longer and more than one can be occurring in the same basin at the same time, the names are thought to reduce the confusion about what storm is being described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name?_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-79">[79]</a></sup> Names are assigned in order from predetermined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lists_of_tropical_cyclone_names" title="Lists of tropical cyclone names">lists</a> with one, three, or ten-minute sustained wind speeds of more than 65 km/h (40 mph) depending on which basin it originates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATL/EPAC_TCOP_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATL/EPAC_TCOP-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIO_TCOP_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWIO_TCOP_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-77">[77]</a></sup> However, standards vary from basin to basin with some tropical depressions named in the Western Pacific, while tropical cyclones have to have a significant amount of gale-force winds occurring around the center before they are named within the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWIO_TCOP_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup> The names of significant tropical cyclones in the North Atlantic Ocean, Pacific Ocean, and Australian region are retired from the naming lists and replaced with another name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATL/EPAC_TCOP_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-ATL/EPAC_TCOP-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPAC_TCOP_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones that develop around the world are assigned an identification code consisting of a two-digit number and suffix letter by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Warning_center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Warning center (page does not exist)">warning centers</a> that monitor them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OFCM_NHOP_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-OFCM_NHOP-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eye_and_center">Eye and center</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">Eye (cyclone)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg"><img alt="Lightning illuminating an eyewall" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3280" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg/220px-Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg/330px-Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg/440px-Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bansi_2015-01-12_210417Z_-_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thunderstorm activity in the eyewall of <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9315_South-West_Indian_Ocean_cyclone_season#Very_Intense_Tropical_Cyclone_Bansi" title="2014–15 South-West Indian Ocean cyclone season">Cyclone Bansi</a> as seen from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, on January 12, 2015</div></div></div>
<p>At the center of a mature tropical cyclone, air sinks rather than rises. For a sufficiently strong storm, air may sink over a layer deep enough to suppress cloud formation, thereby creating a clear "<a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a>". Weather in the eye is normally calm and free of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective_storm" title="Convective storm">convective clouds</a>, although the sea may be extremely violent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JetStream_structure_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-JetStream_structure-82">[82]</a></sup> The eye is normally circular and is typically 30–65 km (19–40 mi) in diameter, though eyes as small as 3 km (1.9 mi) and as large as 370 km (230 mi) have been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilmaTCR_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-WilmaTCR-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR_1996_AHS_summary_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cloudy outer edge of the eye is called the "eyewall". The eyewall typically expands outward with height, resembling an arena football stadium; this phenomenon is sometimes referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)#Stadium_effect" title="Eye (cyclone)">stadium effect</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR_1996_AHS_summary_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-84">[84]</a></sup> The eyewall is where the greatest wind speeds are found, air rises most rapidly, clouds reach their highest <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a>, and precipitation is the heaviest. The heaviest wind damage occurs where a tropical cyclone's eyewall passes over land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JetStream_structure_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-JetStream_structure-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a weaker storm, the eye may be obscured by the <a href="/wiki/Central_dense_overcast" title="Central dense overcast">central dense overcast</a>, which is the upper-level cirrus shield that is associated with a concentrated area of strong thunderstorm activity near the center of a tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDO_AMS_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDO_AMS-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The eyewall may vary over time in the form of <a href="/wiki/Eyewall_replacement_cycle" title="Eyewall replacement cycle">eyewall replacement cycles</a>, particularly in intense tropical cyclones. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainbands" title="Rainbands">Outer rainbands</a> can organize into an outer ring of thunderstorms that slowly moves inward, which is believed to rob the primary eyewall of moisture and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>. When the primary eyewall weakens, the tropical cyclone weakens temporarily. The outer eyewall eventually replaces the primary one at the end of the cycle, at which time the storm may return to its original intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_D8_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_D8-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<p>There are a variety of metrics commonly used to measure storm size. The most common metrics include the radius of maximum wind, the radius of 34-knot (17 m/s; 63 km/h; 39 mph) wind (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Gale" title="Gale">gale force</a>), the radius of outermost closed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobar_(meteorology)" title="Isobar (meteorology)">isobar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Radius_of_outermost_closed_isobar" title="Radius of outermost closed isobar">ROCI</a>), and the radius of vanishing wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL-88">[88]</a></sup> An additional metric is the radius at which the cyclone's relative <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a> field decreases to 1×10<sup>−5</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR_1996_AHS_summary_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; font-size: 92%; margin: 1em 0 1em 1em;">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background: #ccf;">Size descriptions of tropical cyclones
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>ROCI (Diameter)</th>
<th>Type
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Less than 2 degrees latitude</td>
<td>Very small/minor
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2 to 3 degrees of latitude</td>
<td>Small
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3 to 6 degrees of latitude</td>
<td>Medium/Average/Normal
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>6 to 8 degrees of latitude</td>
<td>Large
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Over 8 degrees of latitude</td>
<td>Very large<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JTWCsize_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-JTWCsize-89">[89]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>On Earth, tropical cyclones span a large range of sizes, from 100–2,000 km (62–1,243 mi) as measured by the radius of vanishing wind. They are largest on average in the northwest Pacific Ocean basin and smallest in the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merrill_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merrill-90">[90]</a></sup> If the radius of outermost closed isobar is less than two <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">degrees of latitude</a> (222 km (138 mi)), then the cyclone is "very small" or a "midget". A radius of 3–6 latitude degrees (333–670 km (207–416 mi)) is considered "average sized". "Very large" tropical cyclones have a radius of greater than 8 degrees (888 km (552 mi)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JTWCsize_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-JTWCsize-89">[89]</a></sup> Observations indicate that size is only weakly correlated to variables such as storm intensity (i.e. maximum wind speed), radius of maximum wind, latitude, and maximum potential intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merrill_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merrill-90">[90]</a></sup> Typhoon Tip is the largest cyclone on record, with tropical storm-force winds 2,170 km (1,350 mi) in diameter. The smallest storm on record is <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Marco_(2008)" title="Tropical Storm Marco (2008)">Tropical Storm Marco (2008)</a>, which generated tropical storm-force winds only 37 km (23 mi) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Movement">Movement</span></h2>
<p>The movement of a tropical cyclone (i.e. its "track") is typically approximated as the sum of two terms: "steering" by the background environmental wind and "beta drift".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_JAS_1983_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_JAS_1983-92">[92]</a></sup> Some tropical cyclones can move across large distances, such as <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_John_(1994)" title="Hurricane John (1994)">Hurricane John</a>, the longest-lasting tropical cyclone on record, which traveled 13,280 km (8,250 mi), the longest track of any Northern Hemisphere tropical cyclone, over its 31-day lifespan in <a href="/wiki/1994_Pacific_hurricane_season" title="1994 Pacific hurricane season">1994</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-john94_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-john94-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faqe7_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-faqe7-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_steering">Environmental steering</span></h3>
<p>Environmental steering is the primary influence on the motion of tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-95">[95]</a></sup> It represents the movement of the storm due to prevailing winds and other wider environmental conditions, similar to "leaves carried along by a stream".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G6_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G6-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Physically, the winds, or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Flow_field&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Flow field (page does not exist)">flow field</a>, in the vicinity of a tropical cyclone may be treated as having two parts: the flow associated with the storm itself, and the large-scale background flow of the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-95">[95]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones can be treated as local maxima of <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a> suspended within the large-scale background flow of the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995-97">[97]</a></sup> In this way, tropical cyclone motion may be represented to first-order as <a href="/wiki/Advection" title="Advection">advection</a> of the storm by the local <a href="/wiki/Environmental_flow" title="Environmental flow">environmental flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_and_Elsberry_1990_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carr_and_Elsberry_1990-98">[98]</a></sup> This environmental flow is termed the "steering flow" and is the dominant influence on tropical cyclone motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-95">[95]</a></sup> The strength and direction of the steering flow can be approximated as a vertical integration of the winds blowing horizontally in the cyclone's vicinity, weighted by the altitude at which those winds are occurring. Because winds can vary with height, determining the steering flow precisely can be difficult.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pressure_altitude" title="Pressure altitude">pressure altitude</a> at which the background winds are most correlated with a tropical cyclone's motion is known as the "steering level".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995-97">[97]</a></sup> The motion of stronger tropical cyclones is more correlated with the background flow averaged across a thicker portion of <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> compared to weaker tropical cyclones whose motion is more correlated with the background flow averaged across a narrower extent of the lower troposphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Velden_and_Leslie_1991_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Velden_and_Leslie_1991-99">[99]</a></sup> When wind shear and <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> release is present, tropical cyclones tend to move towards regions where <a href="/wiki/Potential_vorticity" title="Potential vorticity">potential vorticity</a> is increasing most quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climatologically, tropical cyclones are steered primarily westward by the east-to-west <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a> on the equatorial side of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a>—a persistent high-pressure area over the world's subtropical oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G6_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G6-96">[96]</a></sup> In the tropical North Atlantic and Northeast Pacific oceans, the trade winds steer <a href="/wiki/Tropical_wave" title="Tropical wave">tropical easterly waves</a> westward from the African coast toward the Caribbean Sea, North America, and ultimately into the central Pacific Ocean before the waves dampen out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_A4_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_A4-101">[101]</a></sup> These waves are the precursors to many tropical cyclones within this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWR_Avila_1995_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWR_Avila_1995-102">[102]</a></sup> In contrast, in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and Western Pacific in both hemispheres, tropical <a href="/wiki/Cyclogenesis" title="Cyclogenesis">cyclogenesis</a> is influenced less by tropical easterly waves and more by the seasonal movement of the Intertropical Convergence Zone and the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">monsoon trough</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-103">[103]</a></sup> Other weather systems such as mid-latitude <a href="/wiki/Trough_(meteorology)" title="Trough (meteorology)">troughs</a> and broad monsoon gyres can also influence tropical cyclone motion by modifying the steering flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Velden_and_Leslie_1991_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Velden_and_Leslie_1991-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_and_Elsberry_1995_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carr_and_Elsberry_1995-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beta_drift">Beta drift</span></h3>
<p>In addition to environmental steering, a tropical cyclone will tend to drift poleward and westward, a motion known as "beta drift".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_et_al._1998_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_et_al._1998-105">[105]</a></sup> This motion is due to the superposition of a vortex, such as a tropical cyclone, onto an environment in which the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a> varies with latitude, such as on a sphere or <a href="/wiki/Beta_plane" title="Beta plane">beta plane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_1983_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_1983-106">[106]</a></sup> The magnitude of the component of tropical cyclone motion associated with the beta drift ranges between 1–3 m/s (3.6–10.8 km/h; 2.2–6.7 mph) and tends to be larger for more intense tropical cyclones and at higher latitudes. It is induced indirectly by the storm itself as a result of feedback between the cyclonic flow of the storm and its environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiorino_and_Elsberry_1989_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiorino_and_Elsberry_1989-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_et_al._1998_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_et_al._1998-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Physically, the cyclonic circulation of the storm advects environmental air poleward east of center and equatorial west of center. Because air must conserve its <a href="/wiki/Absolute_angular_momentum" title="Absolute angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, this flow configuration induces a cyclonic gyre equatorward and westward of the storm center and an anticyclonic gyre poleward and eastward of the storm center. The combined flow of these gyres acts to advect the storm slowly poleward and westward. This effect occurs even if there is zero environmental flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_and_Wang_1994_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li_and_Wang_1994-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willoughby_1990_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willoughby_1990-109">[109]</a></sup> Due to a direct dependence of the beta drift on angular momentum, the size of a tropical cyclone can impact the influence of beta drift on its motion; beta drift imparts a greater influence on the movement of larger tropical cyclones than that of smaller ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_and_Lackmann_2009_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_and_Lackmann_2009-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun_et_al._2015_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun_et_al._2015-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_storm_interaction">Multiple storm interaction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fujiwhara_effect" title="Fujiwhara effect">Fujiwhara effect</a></div>
<p>A third component of motion that occurs relatively infrequently involves the interaction of multiple tropical cyclones. When two cyclones approach one another, their centers will begin orbiting cyclonically about a point between the two systems. Depending on their separation distance and strength, the two vortices may simply orbit around one another, or else may spiral into the center point and merge. When the two vortices are of unequal size, the larger vortex will tend to dominate the interaction, and the smaller vortex will orbit around it. This phenomenon is called the Fujiwhara effect, after <a href="/wiki/Sakuhei_Fujiwhara" title="Sakuhei Fujiwhara">Sakuhei Fujiwhara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_with_the_mid-latitude_westerlies">Interaction with the mid-latitude westerlies</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ioke_2006_track.png"><img alt="Path of a tropical cyclone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1993" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ioke_2006_track.png/190px-Ioke_2006_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ioke_2006_track.png/285px-Ioke_2006_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ioke_2006_track.png/380px-Ioke_2006_track.png 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ioke_2006_track.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Storm track of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ioke" title="Hurricane Ioke">Typhoon Ioke</a>, showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recurvature" title="Recurvature">recurvature</a> off the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_coastline" title="Japanese coastline">Japanese coast</a> in <a href="/wiki/2006_Pacific_hurricane_season" title="2006 Pacific hurricane season">2006</a></div></div></div>
<p>Though a tropical cyclone typically moves from east to west in the tropics, its track may shift poleward and eastward either as it moves west of the subtropical ridge axis or else if it interacts with the mid-latitude flow, such as the <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet stream</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclone</a>. This motion, termed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recurvature" title="Recurvature">recurvature</a>", commonly occurs near the western edge of the major ocean basins, where the jet stream typically has a poleward component and extratropical cyclones are common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> An example of tropical cyclone recurvature was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoon_Ioke" title="Typhoon Ioke">Typhoon Ioke</a> in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPHC_Ioke_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPHC_Ioke-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_regions_and_warning_centers">Formation regions and warning centers</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins" title="Tropical cyclone basins">Tropical cyclone basins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre" title="Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre">Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre</a></div>

<p>The majority of tropical cyclones each year form in one of seven tropical cyclone basins, which are monitored by a variety of meteorological services and warning centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Ten of these warning centres worldwide are designated as either a <a href="/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre" title="Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre">Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre#Tropical_cyclones" title="Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre">Tropical Cyclone Warning Centre</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organisation" title="World Meteorological Organisation">World Meteorological Organisation</a>'s (WMO) tropical cyclone programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> These warning centres issue advisories which provide basic information and cover a systems present, forecast position, movement and intensity, in their designated areas of responsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Meteorological services around the world are generally responsible for issuing warnings for their own country, however, there are exceptions, as the United States National Hurricane Center and Fiji Meteorological Service issue alerts, watches and warnings for various island nations in their areas of responsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-9"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup> The United States <a href="/wiki/Joint_Typhoon_Warning_Center" title="Joint Typhoon Warning Center">Joint Typhoon Warning Center</a> and Fleet Weather Center also publicly issue warnings, about tropical cyclones on behalf of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Government" title="United States Government">United States Government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Navy" title="Brazilian Navy">Brazilian Navy</a> Hydrographic Center names <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone" title="South Atlantic tropical cyclone">South Atlantic tropical cyclones</a>, however the South Atlantic is not a major basin, and not an official basin according to the WMO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-names_2011_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-names_2011-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparations">Preparations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_preparedness" title="Tropical cyclone preparedness">Tropical cyclone preparedness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_engineering" title="Tropical cyclone engineering">Tropical cyclone engineering</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x,
