You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tropical_cyclone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Ahead of the formal season starting, people are urged to prepare for the effects of a tropical cyclone by politicians and weather forecasters, amongst others. They prepare by determining their risk to the different types of weather, tropical cyclones cause, checking their insurance coverage and emergency supplies, as well as determining where to evacuate to if needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReadyGov_Preparedness_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReadyGov_Preparedness-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray_et_al._2019_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray_et_al._2019-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrissey_and_Reser_2003_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrissey_and_Reser_2003-118">[118]</a></sup> When a tropical cyclone develops and is forecast to impact land, each member nation of the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> issues various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_watches_and_warnings" title="Tropical cyclone watches and warnings">watches and warnings</a> to cover the expected impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_TC_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO_TC-119">[119]</a></sup> However, there are some exceptions with the United States National Hurricane Center and Fiji Meteorological Service responsible for issuing or recommending warnings for other nations in their area of responsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AboutFMS_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-AboutFMS-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_NHC_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-About_NHC-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAIV2017_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAIV2017-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2–4">: 2–4 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts">Impacts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_tropical_cyclones" title="Effects of tropical cyclones">Effects of tropical cyclones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_effects_by_region" title="Tropical cyclone effects by region">Tropical cyclone effects by region</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_phenomena_caused_or_worsened_by_tropical_cyclones">Natural phenomena caused or worsened by tropical cyclones</span></h3>
<p>Tropical cyclones out at sea cause large waves, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_rain_(meteorology)" title="Heavy rain (meteorology)">heavy rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a> and high winds, disrupting international shipping and, at times, causing shipwrecks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18cva_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-18cva-123">[123]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones stir up water, leaving a cool wake behind them, which causes the region to be less favorable for subsequent tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Cooling_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Cooling-18">[18]</a></sup> On land, strong <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a> can damage or destroy vehicles, buildings, bridges, and other outside objects, turning loose debris into deadly flying projectiles. The <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a>, or the increase in sea level due to the cyclone, is typically the worst effect from landfalling tropical cyclones, historically resulting in 90% of tropical cyclone deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Mahina" title="Cyclone Mahina">Cyclone Mahina</a> produced the highest storm surge on record, 13 m (43 ft), at <a href="/wiki/Bathurst_Bay" title="Bathurst Bay">Bathurst Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, in March 1899.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Other ocean-based hazards that tropical cyclones produce are <a href="/wiki/Rip_current" title="Rip current">rip currents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Undertow_(water_waves)" title="Undertow (water waves)">undertow</a>. These hazards can occur hundreds of kilometers (hundreds of miles) away from the center of a cyclone, even if other weather conditions are favorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
The broad rotation of a landfalling tropical cyclone, and vertical wind shear at its periphery, spawns <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tropical_cyclone-spawned_tornadoes" title="List of tropical cyclone-spawned tornadoes">tornadoes</a>. Tornadoes can also be spawned as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall_mesovortices" title="Eyewall mesovortices">eyewall mesovortices</a>, which persist until landfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_L6_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_L6-128">[128]</a></sup> Hurricane Ivan produced 120 tornadoes, more than any other tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Lightning activity is produced within tropical cyclones; this activity is more intense within stronger storms and closer to and within the storm's eyewall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones can increase the amount of snowfall a region experiences by delivering additional moisture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Wildfires can be worsened when a nearby storm fans their flames with its strong winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_property_and_human_life">Impact on property and human life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula,_TX.jpg"><img alt="total collapse of houses, cars and facilities" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1257" data-file-width="2718" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula,_TX.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ike" title="Hurricane Ike">Hurricane Ike</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bolivar_Peninsula,_Texas" title="Bolivar Peninsula, Texas">Bolivar Peninsula, Texas</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tropical cyclones regularly affect the coastlines of most of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s major bodies of water along the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a> oceans. Tropical cyclones have caused significant destruction and loss of human life, resulting in about 2 million deaths since the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-red_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-red-135">[135]</a></sup> Large areas of standing water caused by flooding lead to <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a>, as well as contributing to <a href="/wiki/Mosquito-borne_disease" title="Mosquito-borne disease">mosquito-borne illnesses</a>. Crowded evacuees in <a href="/wiki/Emergency_shelter" title="Emergency shelter">shelters</a> increase the risk of disease propagation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-124">[124]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones significantly interrupt infrastructure, leading to <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">power outages</a>, bridge and road destruction, and the hampering of reconstruction efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Power_failures_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Power_failures-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Winds and water from storms can damage or destroy homes, buildings, and other manmade structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones destroy agriculture, kill livestock, and prevent access to marketplaces for both buyers and sellers; both of these result in financial losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Powerful cyclones that make <a href="/wiki/Landfall" title="Landfall">landfall</a> – moving from the ocean to over land – are some of the most impactful, although that is not always the case. An average of 86 tropical cyclones of tropical storm intensity form annually worldwide, with 47 reaching hurricane or typhoon strength, and 20 becoming intense tropical cyclones, super typhoons, or major hurricanes (at least of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_Hurricane_Scale" title="Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 3</a> intensity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landsea_variability_table_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landsea_variability_table-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, tropical cyclones can originate from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_wave" title="Tropical wave">tropical waves</a> generated over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> or otherwise strike the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Idai" title="Cyclone Idai">Cyclone Idai</a> in March 2019 hit central <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, becoming the deadliest tropical cyclone on record in Africa, with 1,302 fatalities, and damage estimated at US$2.2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Catastrophe_Recap_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Catastrophe_Recap-148">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="Réunion">Réunion</a> island, located east of Southern Africa, experiences some of the wettest tropical cyclones on record. In January 1980, <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Hyacinthe" title="Cyclone Hyacinthe">Cyclone Hyacinthe</a> produced 6,083 mm (239.5 in) of rain over 15 days, which was the largest rain total recorded from a tropical cyclone on record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, tropical cyclones from the Indian and Pacific oceans regularly affect some of the most populated countries on Earth. In 1970, <a href="/wiki/1970_Bhola_cyclone" title="1970 Bhola cyclone">a cyclone</a> struck <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, then known as East Pakistan, producing a 6.1 m (20 ft) storm surge that killed at least 300,000 people; this made it the deadliest tropical cyclone on record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dead_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-dead-152">[152]</a></sup> In October 2019, <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Hagibis" title="Typhoon Hagibis">Typhoon Hagibis</a> struck the <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a> and inflicted US$15 billion in damage, making it the costliest storm on record in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The islands that comprise <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> to <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a>, are routinely affected by tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/1973_Flores_cyclone" title="1973 Flores cyclone">a cyclone</a> struck the island of <a href="/wiki/Flores" title="Flores">Flores</a> in April 1973, killing 1,653 people, making it the deadliest tropical cyclone recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane" title="Atlantic hurricane">Atlantic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_hurricanes" title="Pacific hurricanes">Pacific hurricanes</a> regularly affect <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Katrina</a> in 2005 and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Harvey" title="Hurricane Harvey">Harvey</a> in 2017 are the country's costliest ever natural disasters, with monetary damage estimated at US$125 billion. Katrina struck <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and largely destroyed the city of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvey_TCR_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvey_TCR-160">[160]</a></sup> while Harvey caused significant flooding in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> after it dropped 60.58 in (1,539 mm) of rainfall; this was the highest rainfall total on record in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvey_TCR_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harvey_TCR-160">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> is rarely affected by tropical cyclones; however, the continent regularly encounters storms after they transitioned into <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclones</a>. Only one tropical depression – <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Vince" title="Hurricane Vince">Vince</a> in 2005 – struck <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> and only one <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone" title="Subtropical cyclone">subtropical cyclone</a> – <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_Storm_Alpha_(2020)" title="Subtropical Storm Alpha (2020)">Subtropical Storm Alpha</a> in 2020 – struck <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Occasionally, there are <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_tropical-like_cyclone" title="Mediterranean tropical-like cyclone">tropical-like cyclones</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The northern portion of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> experiences occasional tropical cyclones, with 173 fatalities from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bret_(1993)" title="Tropical Storm Bret (1993)">Tropical Storm Bret</a> in August 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Ocean" title="South Atlantic Ocean">South Atlantic Ocean</a> is generally inhospitable to the formation of a tropical storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> However, in March 2004, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Catarina" title="Hurricane Catarina">Hurricane Catarina</a> struck southeastern <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> as the first hurricane on record in the South Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h3>
<p>Although cyclones take an enormous toll in lives and personal property, they may be important factors in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> regimes of places they impact, as they may bring much-needed precipitation to otherwise dry regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_EPac_outlook_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005_EPac_outlook-168">[168]</a></sup> Their precipitation may also alleviate drought conditions by restoring soil moisture, though one study focused on the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">Southeastern United States</a> suggested tropical cyclones did not offer significant drought recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DontFix_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-DontFix-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoo_et_al._(2015)_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yoo_et_al._(2015)-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kam_et_al._(2013)_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kam_et_al._(2013)-171">[171]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones also help maintain the global heat balance by moving warm, moist tropical air to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">middle latitudes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth" title="Polar regions of Earth">polar regions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JetStream_introduction_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-JetStream_introduction-172">[172]</a></sup> and by regulating the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> through <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The storm surge and winds of hurricanes may be destructive to human-made structures, but they also stir up the waters of coastal <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, which are typically important <a href="/wiki/Fish_reproduction" title="Fish reproduction">fish breeding</a> locales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopherson_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christopherson-174">[174]</a></sup> Ecosystems, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltmarsh" title="Saltmarsh">saltmarshes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mangrove_forest" title="Mangrove forest">Mangrove forests</a>, can be severely damaged or destroyed by tropical cyclones, which erode land and destroy vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones can cause harmful <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algae blooms</a> to form in bodies of water by increasing the amount of nutrients available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oil+Algae_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oil+Algae-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Insect populations can decrease in both quantity and diversity after the passage of storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Strong winds associated with tropical cyclones and their remnants are capable of felling thousands of trees, causing damage to forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>When hurricanes surge upon shore from the ocean, salt is introduced to many freshwater areas and raises the <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> levels too high for some habitats to withstand. Some are able to cope with the salt and recycle it back into the ocean, but others can not release the extra surface water quickly enough or do not have a large enough freshwater source to replace it. Because of this, some species of plants and vegetation die due to the excess salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> In addition, hurricanes can carry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxins" title="Toxins">toxins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">acids</a> onshore when they make landfall. The floodwater can pick up the toxins from different spills and contaminate the land that it passes over. These toxins are harmful to the people and animals in the area, as well as the environment around them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones can cause <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> by damaging or destroying pipelines and storage facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oil+Algae_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oil+Algae-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oil_+_Chemicals_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oil_+_Chemicals-185">[185]</a></sup> Similarly, chemical spills have been reported when chemical and processing facilities were damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oil_+_Chemicals_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oil_+_Chemicals-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Waterways have become contaminated with toxic levels of metals such as <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> during tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_+_Mercury_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion_+_Mercury-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tropical cyclones can have an extensive effect on geography, such as creating or destroying land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_created_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Land_created-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Destroyed_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Land_Destroyed-191">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Bebe" title="Cyclone Bebe">Cyclone Bebe</a> increased the size of <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a> island, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funafuti_Atoll" title="Funafuti Atoll">Funafuti Atoll</a>, by nearly 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_created_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Land_created-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Walaka" title="Hurricane Walaka">Hurricane Walaka</a> destroyed the small <a href="/wiki/East_Island,_Hawaii" title="East Island, Hawaii">East Island</a> in 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Destroyed_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Land_Destroyed-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> which destroyed the habitat for the endangered <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_monk_seal" title="Hawaiian monk seal">Hawaiian monk seal</a>, as well as, threatened <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">Landslides</a> frequently occur during tropical cyclones and can vastly alter landscapes; some storms are capable of causing hundreds to tens of thousands of landslides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Storms can erode coastlines over an extensive area and transport the sediment to other locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_+_Mercury_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion_+_Mercury-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Response">Response</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tropical_cyclone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_response" title="Tropical cyclone response">Tropical cyclone response</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_(49091289561).jpg"><img alt="View of tropical cyclone damage from a helicopter" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg/220px-DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg/330px-DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg/440px-DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_(49091289561).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief efforts for <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Dorian" title="Hurricane Dorian">Hurricane Dorian</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">the Bahamas</a></div></div></div>
<p>Hurricane response is the <a href="/wiki/Disaster_response" title="Disaster response">disaster response</a> after a hurricane. Activities performed by hurricane responders include assessment, restoration, and demolition of buildings; removal of <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> and waste; repairs to land-based and maritime <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a>; and public health services including <a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">search and rescue</a> operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Hurricane response requires coordination between federal, tribal, state, local, and private entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Voluntary_Organizations_Active_in_Disaster" title="National Voluntary Organizations Active in Disaster">National Voluntary Organizations Active in Disaster</a>, potential response volunteers should affiliate with established organizations and should not self-deploy, so that proper training and support can be provided to mitigate the danger and stress of response work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hurricane responders face many hazards. Hurricane responders may be exposed to chemical and biological contaminants including stored chemicals, <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpse" title="Corpse">human remains</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indoor_mold" title="Indoor mold">mold</a> growth encouraged by flooding,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> that may be present in older buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-208">[208]</a></sup> Common injuries arise from <a href="/wiki/Falling_(accident)" title="Falling (accident)">falls</a> from heights, such as from a ladder or from level surfaces; from <a href="/wiki/Electrocution" title="Electrocution">electrocution</a> in flooded areas, including from <a href="/wiki/Backfeeding" title="Backfeeding">backfeed</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portable_generator" title="Portable generator">portable generators</a>; or from <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">motor vehicle accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shift_work" title="Shift work">Long and irregular shifts</a> may lead to <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatigue" title="Fatigue">fatigue</a>, increasing the risk of injuries, and workers may experience <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">mental stress associated with a traumatic incident</a>. Additionally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_stress" title="Heat stress">heat stress</a> is a concern as workers are often exposed to hot and humid temperatures, wear protective clothing and equipment, and have physically difficult tasks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_205-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climatology">Climatology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_by_year" title="Tropical cyclones by year">Tropical cyclones by year</a></div>
<p>Tropical cyclones have occurred around the world for millennia. Reanalyses and research are being undertaken to extend the historical record, through the usage of <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy data</a> such as overwash deposits, <a href="/wiki/Beach_ridge" title="Beach ridge">beach ridges</a> and historical documents such as diaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6000_WA_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-6000_WA-3">[3]</a></sup> Major tropical cyclones leave traces in <a href="/wiki/Overwash" title="Overwash">overwash records</a> and shell layers in some coastal areas, which have been used to gain insight into hurricane activity over the past thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-210">[210]</a></sup> Sediment records in Western Australia suggest an intense tropical cyclone in the <a href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BC" title="4th millennium BC">4th millennium BC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6000_WA_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-6000_WA-3">[3]</a></sup> Proxy records based on <a href="/wiki/Paleotempestology" title="Paleotempestology">paleotempestological</a> research have revealed that major hurricane activity along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> coast varies on timescales of centuries to millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu1999_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liu1999-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LiuFearn2000_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-LiuFearn2000-212">[212]</a></sup> In the year 957, a powerful typhoon struck <a href="/wiki/South_China" title="South China">southern China</a>, killing around 10,000 people due to flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">Spanish colonization of Mexico</a> described "tempestades" in 1730,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calif_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-calif-214">[214]</a></sup> although the official record for Pacific hurricanes only dates to 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HURDAT_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-HURDAT-215">[215]</a></sup> In the south-west Indian Ocean, the tropical cyclone record goes back to 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> In 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_reanalysis_project" title="Atlantic hurricane reanalysis project">Atlantic hurricane reanalysis project</a> examined and analyzed the historical record of tropical cyclones in the Atlantic back to 1851, extending the existing database from 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before satellite imagery became available during the 20th century, many of these systems went undetected unless it impacted land or a ship encountered it by chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Often in part because of the threat of hurricanes, many coastal regions had sparse population between major ports until the advent of automobile tourism; therefore, the most severe portions of hurricanes striking the coast may have gone unmeasured in some instances. The combined effects of ship destruction and remote landfall severely limit the number of intense hurricanes in the official record before the era of hurricane reconnaissance aircraft and satellite meteorology. Although the record shows a distinct increase in the number and strength of intense hurricanes, therefore, experts regard the early data as suspect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BOM_TC_Guide_1.3_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-BOM_TC_Guide_1.3-218">[218]</a></sup> The ability of climatologists to make a long-term analysis of tropical cyclones is limited by the amount of reliable historical data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> During the 1940s, routine aircraft reconnaissance started in both the Atlantic and Western Pacific basin during the mid-1940s, which provided ground truth data, however, early flights were only made once or twice a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Polar-orbiting weather satellites were first launched by the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> in 1960 but were not declared operational until 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> However, it took several years for some of the warning centres to take advantage of this new viewing platform and develop the expertise to associate satellite signatures with storm position and intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each year on average, around 80 to 90 named tropical cyclones form around the world, of which over half develop hurricane-force winds of 65 kn (120 km/h; 75 mph) or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Guide_2017_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Worldwide, tropical cyclone activity peaks in late summer, when the difference between temperatures aloft and <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperatures</a> is the greatest. However, each particular basin has its own seasonal patterns. On a worldwide scale, May is the least active month, while September is the most active month. November is the only month in which all the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins" title="Tropical cyclone basins">tropical cyclone basins</a> are in season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G1_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-220">[220]</a></sup> In the Northern <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, a distinct <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Atlantic hurricane season">cyclone season</a> occurs from June 1 to November 30, sharply peaking from late August through September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G1_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-220">[220]</a></sup> The statistical peak of the Atlantic hurricane season is September 10. The Northeast Pacific Ocean has a broader period of activity, but in a similar time frame to the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_Atl_climatology_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_Atl_climatology-221">[221]</a></sup> The Northwest Pacific sees tropical cyclones year-round, with a minimum in February and March and a peak in early September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G1_220-2"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-220">[220]</a></sup> In the North Indian basin, storms are most common from April to December, with peaks in May and November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G1_220-3"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-220">[220]</a></sup> In the Southern Hemisphere, the tropical cyclone year begins on July 1 and runs all year-round encompassing the tropical cyclone seasons, which run from November 1 until the end of April, with peaks in mid-February to early March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_G1_220-4"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_78-10"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of various <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">modes of variability</a> in the climate system, <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño–Southern Oscillation">El Niño–Southern Oscillation</a> has the largest impact on tropical cyclone activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-222">[222]</a></sup> Most tropical cyclones form on the side of the subtropical ridge closer to the equator, then move poleward past the ridge axis before recurving into the main belt of the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a> position shifts due to El Niño, so will the preferred tropical cyclone tracks. Areas west of Japan and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> tend to experience much fewer September–November tropical cyclone impacts during <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> and neutral years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-China-224">[224]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a> years, the formation of tropical cyclones, along with the subtropical ridge position, shifts westward across the western Pacific Ocean, which increases the landfall threat to China and much greater intensity in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-China-224">[224]</a></sup> The Atlantic Ocean experiences depressed activity due to increased vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> across the region during El Niño years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones are further influenced by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Meridional_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Atlantic Meridional Mode (page does not exist)">Atlantic Meridional Mode</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-biennial_oscillation" title="Quasi-biennial oscillation">Quasi-biennial oscillation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Madden%E2%80%93Julian_oscillation" title="Madden–Julian oscillation">Madden–Julian oscillation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_climate_change">Influence of climate change</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_and_climate_change" title="Tropical cyclones and climate change">Tropical cyclones and climate change</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg/290px-1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg/435px-1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg/580px-1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 20-year average of the number of annual Category 4 and 5 hurricanes in the Atlantic region has approximately doubled since the year 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_20220929_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes_20220929-231">[231]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> can affect tropical cyclones in a variety of ways: an intensification of rainfall and wind speed, a decrease in overall frequency, an increase in the frequency of very intense storms and a poleward extension of where the cyclones reach maximum intensity are among the possible consequences of human-induced climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-232">[232]</a></sup> Tropical cyclones use warm, moist air as their fuel. As climate change is <a href="/wiki/Ocean_heat_content" title="Ocean heat content">warming ocean temperatures</a>, there is potentially more of this fuel available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Between 1979 and 2017, there was a global increase in the proportion of tropical cyclones of Category 3 and higher on the <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">Saffir–Simpson scale</a>. The trend was most clear in the North Atlantic and in the Southern Indian Ocean. In the North Pacific, tropical cyclones have been moving poleward into colder waters and there was no increase in intensity over this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> With 2 °C (3.6 °F) warming, a greater percentage (+13%) of tropical cyclones are expected to reach Category 4 and 5 strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-232">[232]</a></sup> A 2019 study indicates that climate change has been driving the observed trend of <a href="/wiki/Rapid_intensification" title="Rapid intensification">rapid intensification</a> of tropical cyclones in the Atlantic basin. Rapidly intensifying cyclones are hard to forecast and therefore pose additional risk to coastal communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>Warmer air can hold more water vapor: the theoretical maximum water vapor content is given by the <a href="/wiki/Clausius%E2%80%93Clapeyron_relation" title="Clausius–Clapeyron relation">Clausius–Clapeyron relation</a>, which yields ≈7% increase in water vapor in the atmosphere per 1 °C (1.8 °F) warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knutson_2013_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-knutson_2013-237">[237]</a></sup> All models that were assessed in a 2019 review paper show a future increase of rainfall rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-232">[232]</a></sup> Additional <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a> will increase storm surge levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-239">[239]</a></sup> It is plausible that extreme <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">wind waves</a> see an increase as a consequence of changes in tropical cyclones, further exacerbating storm surge dangers to coastal communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-240">[240]</a></sup> The compounding effects from floods, storm surge, and terrestrial flooding (rivers) are projected to increase <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">due to global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-239">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is currently no consensus on how climate change will affect the overall frequency of tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-:5-232">[232]</a></sup> A majority of <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">climate models</a> show a decreased frequency in future projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-240">[240]</a></sup> For instance, a 2020 paper comparing nine high-resolution climate models found robust decreases in frequency in the Southern Indian Ocean and the Southern Hemisphere more generally, while finding mixed signals for Northern Hemisphere tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> Observations have shown little change in the overall frequency of tropical cyclones worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> with increased frequency in the North Atlantic and central Pacific, and significant decreases in the southern Indian Ocean and western North Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> There has been a poleward expansion of the latitude at which the maximum intensity of tropical cyclones occurs, which may be associated with climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> In the North Pacific, there may also have been an eastward expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-238">[238]</a></sup> Between 1949 and 2016, there was a slowdown in tropical cyclone translation speeds. It is unclear still to what extent this can be attributed to climate change: climate models do not all show this feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_240-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_forecasting">Observation and forecasting</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_observation" title="Tropical cyclone observation">Tropical cyclone observation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg"><img alt="Aerial view of storm clouds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2160" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg/250px-Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg/375px-Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg/500px-Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sunset view of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Isidore" title="Hurricane Isidore">Hurricane Isidore</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">rainbands</a> photographed at 2,100 m (7,000 ft)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_(N42RF)_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_(9119691512).jpg"><img alt="Head-on view of an airplane" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg/220px-NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg/330px-NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg/440px-NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_(N42RF)_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_(9119691512).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Hurricane_hunters" title="Hurricane hunters">Hurricane Hunter</a>" – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WP-3D_Orion" title="WP-3D Orion">WP-3D Orion</a> is used to go into the <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye of a hurricane</a> for data collection and measurements purposes.</div></div></div>
<p>Intense tropical cyclones pose a particular observation challenge, as they are a dangerous oceanic phenomenon, and <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather stations</a>, being relatively sparse, are rarely available on the site of the storm itself. In general, surface observations are available only if the storm is passing over an island or a coastal area, or if there is a nearby ship. Real-time measurements are usually taken in the periphery of the cyclone, where conditions are less catastrophic and its true strength cannot be evaluated. For this reason, there are teams of meteorologists that move into the path of tropical cyclones to help evaluate their strength at the point of landfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCMP_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCMP-245">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tropical cyclones are tracked by <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather satellites</a> capturing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> images from space, usually at half-hour to quarter-hour intervals. As a storm approaches land, it can be observed by land-based <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a>. Radar plays a crucial role around landfall by showing a storm's location and intensity every several minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPHC_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPHC-246">[246]</a></sup> Other satellites provide information from the perturbations of <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> signals, providing thousands of snapshots per day and capturing atmospheric temperature, pressure, and moisture content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">In situ</a> measurements, in real-time, can be taken by sending specially equipped reconnaissance flights into the cyclone. In the Atlantic basin, these flights are regularly flown by United States government <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_hunters" title="Hurricane hunters">hurricane hunters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurricane_Hunters_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hurricane_Hunters-248">[248]</a></sup> These aircraft fly directly into the cyclone and take direct and remote-sensing measurements. The aircraft also launch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS_dropsonde" title="GPS dropsonde">GPS dropsondes</a> inside the cyclone. These sondes measure temperature, humidity, pressure, and especially winds between flight level and the ocean's surface. A new era in hurricane observation began when a remotely piloted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insitu_Aerosonde" title="Insitu Aerosonde">Aerosonde</a>, a small drone aircraft, was flown through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Ophelia_(2005)" title="Tropical Storm Ophelia (2005)">Tropical Storm Ophelia</a> as it passed <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>'s eastern shore during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2005_Hurricane_season" title="2005 Hurricane season">2005 hurricane season</a>. A similar mission was also completed successfully in the western Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunHerald_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-SunHerald-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png"><img alt="Track errors plotted over time" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1313" data-file-width="1750" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png/250px-NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png/375px-NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png/500px-NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A general decrease in error trends in tropical cyclone path prediction is evident since the 1970s</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecasting">Forecasting</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_track_forecasting" title="Tropical cyclone track forecasting">Tropical cyclone track forecasting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_prediction_model" title="Tropical cyclone prediction model">Tropical cyclone prediction model</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_rainfall_forecasting" title="Tropical cyclone rainfall forecasting">Tropical cyclone rainfall forecasting</a></div>
<p>High-speed computers and sophisticated simulation software allow forecasters to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_prediction_model" title="Tropical cyclone prediction model">computer models</a> that predict tropical cyclone tracks based on the future position and strength of high- and low-pressure systems. Combining forecast models with increased understanding of the forces that act on tropical cyclones, as well as with a wealth of data from Earth-orbiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellites" title="Satellites">satellites</a> and other sensors, scientists have increased the accuracy of track forecasts over recent decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_forecast_verifications_models_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_forecast_verifications_models-250">[250]</a></sup> However, scientists are not as skillful at predicting the intensity of tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_forecast_verifications_Atlantic_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_forecast_verifications_Atlantic-251">[251]</a></sup> The lack of improvement in intensity forecasting is attributed to the complexity of tropical systems and an incomplete understanding of factors that affect their development. New tropical cyclone position and forecast information is available at least every six hours from the various warning centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_RSMC_list_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO_RSMC_list-252">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHCprod_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHCprod-255">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geopotential_height">Geopotential height</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geopotential_height" title="Geopotential height">Geopotential height</a></div>
<p>In meteorology, geopotential heights are used when creating forecasts and analyzing pressure systems. Geopotential heights represent the estimate of the real height of a pressure system above the average sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geo_Height_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geo_Height-257">[257]</a></sup> Geopotential heights for weather are divided up into several levels. The lowest geopotential height level is 850 hPa (25.10 inHg), which represents the lowest 1,500 m (5,000 ft) of the atmosphere. The moisture content, gained by using either the relative humidity or the precipitable water value, is used in creating forecasts for precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> The next level, 700 hPa (20.67 inHg), is at a height of 2,300–3,200 m (7,700–10,500 ft); 700 hPa is regarded as the highest point in the lower atmosphere. At this layer, both vertical movement and moisture levels are used to locate and create forecasts for precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> The middle level of the atmosphere is at 500 hPa (14.76 inHg) or a height of 4,900–6,100 m (16,000–20,000 ft). The 500 hPa level is used for measuring atmospheric vorticity, commonly known as the spin of air. The relative humidity is also analyzed at this height in order to establish where precipitation is likely to materialize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> The next level occurs at 300 hPa (8.859 inHg) or a height of 8,200–9,800 m (27,000–32,000 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> The top-most level is located at 200 hPa (5.906 inHg), which corresponds to a height of 11,000–12,000 m (35,000–41,000 ft). Both the 200 and 300 hPa levels are mainly utilized to locate the jet stream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_cyclone_types">Related cyclone types</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">Cyclone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">Extratropical cyclone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone" title="Subtropical cyclone">Subtropical cyclone</a></div>
<p>In addition to tropical cyclones, there are two other classes of cyclones within the spectrum of cyclone types. These kinds of cyclones, known as <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone" title="Subtropical cyclone">subtropical cyclones</a>, can be stages a tropical cyclone passes through during its <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis" title="Tropical cyclogenesis">formation</a> or dissipation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IWTC5_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-IWTC5-263">[263]</a></sup> An <i>extratropical cyclone</i> is a storm that derives energy from horizontal temperature differences, which are typical in higher latitudes. A tropical cyclone can become extratropical as it moves toward higher latitudes if its energy source changes from heat released by condensation to differences in temperature between air masses; although not as frequently, an extratropical cyclone can transform into a subtropical storm, and from there into a tropical cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-264">[264]</a></sup> From space, extratropical storms have a characteristic "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comma_(punctuation)" title="Comma (punctuation)">comma</a>-shaped" cloud pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L14_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-L14-265">[265]</a></sup> Extratropical cyclones can also be dangerous when their low-pressure centers cause powerful winds and high seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>subtropical cyclone</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> system that has some characteristics of a tropical cyclone and some characteristics of an extratropical cyclone. They can form in a wide band of latitudes, from the equator to 50°. Although subtropical storms rarely have hurricane-force winds, they may become tropical in nature as their cores warm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_A6_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_A6-267">[267]</a></sup>
</p>
